					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">American rapper and actor (born 1969)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"O'Shea Jackson" redirects here. For his son, see <a href="/wiki/O%27Shea_Jackson_Jr." title="O&#39;Shea Jackson Jr.">O'Shea Jackson Jr.</a> For cube-shaped ice, see <a href="/wiki/Ice_cube" title="Ice cube">Ice cube</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Ice_cube_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Ice cube (disambiguation)">Ice cube (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Ice-T" title="Ice-T">Ice-T</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://icecube.com">icecube<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Musical artist</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>O'Shea Jackson Sr.</b> (born June 15, 1969), better known as <b>Ice Cube</b>, is an American rapper, songwriter, actor, and film producer. His lyrics on <a href="/wiki/N.W.A" title="N.W.A">N.W.A</a>'s 1988 album <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton" title="Straight Outta Compton">Straight Outta Compton</a></i> contributed to <a href="/wiki/Gangsta_rap" title="Gangsta rap">gangsta rap</a>'s widespread popularity,<sup id="cite_ref-:2_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:17_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and his <a href="/wiki/Political_rap" class="mw-redirect" title="Political rap">political rap</a> solo albums <i><a href="/wiki/AmeriKKKa%27s_Most_Wanted" title="AmeriKKKa&#39;s Most Wanted">AmeriKKKa's Most Wanted</a></i> (1990), <i><a href="/wiki/Death_Certificate_(album)" title="Death Certificate (album)">Death Certificate</a></i> (1991), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Predator_(Ice_Cube_album)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Predator (Ice Cube album)">The Predator</a></i> (1992) were all critically and commercially successful.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:8_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:11_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> as a member of N.W.A in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A native of Los Angeles, Ice Cube formed his first rap group called <a href="/wiki/C.I.A._(band)" title="C.I.A. (band)">C.I.A.</a> in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-Arts_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arts-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In 1987, with <a href="/wiki/Eazy-E" title="Eazy-E">Eazy-E</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>, he formed the gangsta rap group N.W.A.<sup id="cite_ref-Arts_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arts-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As its lead rapper, he wrote some of Dre's and most of Eazy's lyrics on <i>Straight Outta Compton</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-:2_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> a landmark album that shaped <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_hip_hop" title="West Coast hip hop">West Coast hip hop</a>'s early identity and helped differentiate it from <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_rap" class="mw-redirect" title="East Coast rap">East Coast rap</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:17_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> N.W.A was also known for their violent lyrics, threatening to attack abusive police which stirred controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arts_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arts-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> After a monetary dispute over the group's management by Eazy-E and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Heller" title="Jerry Heller">Jerry Heller</a>, Cube left N.W.A in late 1989, teaming with New York artists and launching a solo rap career.<sup id="cite_ref-Arts_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arts-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ice Cube has also had an active film career since the early 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:16_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He entered cinema by playing Doughboy in director <a href="/wiki/John_Singleton" title="John Singleton">John Singleton</a>'s feature debut <i><a href="/wiki/Boyz_n_the_Hood" title="Boyz n the Hood">Boyz n the Hood</a></i>, a 1991 drama named after a <a href="/wiki/Boyz-n-the-Hood" title="Boyz-n-the-Hood">1987 rap song</a><sup id="cite_ref-:17_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> that Ice Cube wrote.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He also co-wrote and starred in the 1995 comedy film <i><a href="/wiki/Friday_(1995_film)" title="Friday (1995 film)">Friday</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> which spawned a successful franchise and reshaped his public image into a bankable movie star.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He made his directorial debut with the 1998 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Players_Club" title="The Players Club">The Players Club</a></i>, and also produced and curated the film's accompanying <a href="/wiki/The_Players_Club_(soundtrack)" title="The Players Club (soundtrack)">soundtrack</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> As of 2020, he has appeared in about 40 films, including the 1999 war comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Kings_(1999_film)" title="Three Kings (1999 film)">Three Kings</a></i>, family comedies like the <a href="/wiki/Barbershop_(film_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbershop (film series)"><i>Barbershop</i> series</a>, and buddy cop comedies <i><a href="/wiki/21_Jump_Street_(film)" title="21 Jump Street (film)">21 Jump Street</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/22_Jump_Street" title="22 Jump Street">22 Jump Street</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Ride_Along_(film)" title="Ride Along (film)">Ride Along</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He was an executive producer of many of these films, as well as of the 2015 biopic <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton_(film)" title="Straight Outta Compton (film)">Straight Outta Compton</a></i>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ice_Cube_HS_Yearbook.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ice_Cube_HS_Yearbook.jpeg/175px-Ice_Cube_HS_Yearbook.jpeg" decoding="async" width="175" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ice_Cube_HS_Yearbook.jpeg/263px-Ice_Cube_HS_Yearbook.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Ice_Cube_HS_Yearbook.jpeg/350px-Ice_Cube_HS_Yearbook.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="633" data-file-height="914" /></a><figcaption>Ice Cube as a high school senior in 1987</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ice Cube was born O'Shea Jackson in Los Angeles on June 15, 1969, the son of hospital clerk and custodian Doris and machinist and <a href="/wiki/UCLA" class="mw-redirect" title="UCLA">UCLA</a> groundskeeper Hosea Jackson.<sup id="cite_ref-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hiphop.sh_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hiphop.sh-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He has an older brother,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and they had a half-sister who was murdered when Cube was 12.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He is the cousin of fellow rappers <a href="/wiki/Del_tha_Funky_Homosapien" class="mw-redirect" title="Del tha Funky Homosapien">Del tha Funky Homosapien</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kam_(rapper)" title="Kam (rapper)">Kam</a>. He grew up on Van Wick Street in the <a href="/wiki/Westmont,_California" title="Westmont, California">Westmont</a> section of <a href="/wiki/South_Los_Angeles" title="South Los Angeles">South Los Angeles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In ninth grade at <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Preparatory_High_School" class="mw-redirect" title="George Washington Preparatory High School">George Washington Preparatory High School</a> in Los Angeles,<sup id="cite_ref-Fresh_Air_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fresh_Air-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Cube began writing raps after being challenged by his friend "Kiddo" in typewriting class. Kiddo lost.<sup id="cite_ref-under_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-under-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> He has said that his stage name came from his older brother, who "threatened to slam [him] into a freezer and pull [him] out when [he] was an ice cube".<sup id="cite_ref-under_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-under-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cube also attended <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft_Charter_High_School_(Los_Angeles)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Howard Taft Charter High School (Los Angeles)">William Howard Taft High School</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Woodland_Hills,_Los_Angeles" title="Woodland Hills, Los Angeles">Woodland Hills</a> area of Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He was bused 40 miles to the suburban school from his home in a high-crime neighborhood.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In the fall of 1987, soon after he wrote and recorded a few locally successful rap songs with <a href="/wiki/N.W.A" title="N.W.A">N.W.A</a>, he enrolled at the Phoenix Institute of Technology <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BlackCollegian_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlackCollegian-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In 1988, with a diploma in <a href="/wiki/Architectural_drawing" title="Architectural drawing">architectural drafting</a>, he returned to Los Angeles and rejoined N.W.A, but kept a career in architecture drafting as a backup plan.<sup id="cite_ref-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_career">Music career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_work">Early work</span></h3>
<p>In 1986, at the age of 16, Ice Cube began rapping in the trio <a href="/wiki/C.I.A._(band)" title="C.I.A. (band)">C.I.A.</a> but soon joined the newly formed rap group <a href="/wiki/N.W.A" title="N.W.A">N.W.A</a>. He was N.W.A's lead rapper and main ghostwriter on its official debut album, 1988's <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton" title="Straight Outta Compton">Straight Outta Compton</a></i>. Due to a financial dispute, he left the group by the start of 1990. During 1990, his debut solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/AmeriKKKa%27s_Most_Wanted" title="AmeriKKKa&#39;s Most Wanted">AmeriKKKa's Most Wanted</a></i>, found him also leading a featured rap group, <a href="/wiki/Da_Lench_Mob" title="Da Lench Mob">Da Lench Mob</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-chillin_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chillin-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, he helped develop the rapper <a href="/wiki/Yo-Yo_(rapper)" title="Yo-Yo (rapper)">Yo Yo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1986:_C.I.A.">1986: C.I.A.</span></h3>
<p>With friend <a href="/wiki/Sir_Jinx" title="Sir Jinx">Sir Jinx</a>, Ice Cube formed the rap group <a href="/wiki/C.I.A._(group)" class="mw-redirect" title="C.I.A. (group)">C.I.A.</a>, and performed at parties hosted by <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>. Since 1984, Dre was a member of a popular DJ crew, the <a href="/wiki/World_Class_Wreckin%27_Cru" title="World Class Wreckin&#39; Cru">World Class Wreckin' Cru</a>, which by 1985 was also performing and recording electro rap. Dre had Cube help write the Wreckin Cru's hit song "Cabbage Patch". Dre also joined Cube on a side project, a duo called Stereo Crew, which made a 12-inch record, "She's a Skag", released on <a href="/wiki/Epic_Records" title="Epic Records">Epic Records</a> in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1987, C.I.A. released the <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>-produced single "<a href="/wiki/My_Posse" title="My Posse">My Posse</a>". Meanwhile, the Wreckin' Cru's home base was the Eve After Dark nightclub, about a quarter of a mile outside of the city of Compton in Los Angeles county. While Dre was on the turntable, Ice Cube would rap, often parodying other artists' songs. In one instance, Cube's rendition was "My Penis", parodying Run-DMC's "<a href="/wiki/My_Adidas" title="My Adidas">My Adidas</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the nightclub's co-owner and Wreckin' leader <a href="/wiki/Alonzo_Williams" title="Alonzo Williams">Alonzo Williams</a> would recall feeling his reputation damaged by this and asking it not to be repeated.<sup id="cite_ref-Lonzo_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lonzo-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1986.E2.80.931989:_N.W.A."></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1986–1989:_N.W.A.">1986–1989: N.W.A.</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/N.W.A" title="N.W.A">N.W.A</a></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Uncle_Jam%27s_Army_-_Eazy-E_and_N.W.A._1988_Skateland_Concert_Poster.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Uncle_Jam%27s_Army_-_Eazy-E_and_N.W.A._1988_Skateland_Concert_Poster.jpg/150px-Uncle_Jam%27s_Army_-_Eazy-E_and_N.W.A._1988_Skateland_Concert_Poster.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="189" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Uncle_Jam%27s_Army_-_Eazy-E_and_N.W.A._1988_Skateland_Concert_Poster.jpg/225px-Uncle_Jam%27s_Army_-_Eazy-E_and_N.W.A._1988_Skateland_Concert_Poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Uncle_Jam%27s_Army_-_Eazy-E_and_N.W.A._1988_Skateland_Concert_Poster.jpg/300px-Uncle_Jam%27s_Army_-_Eazy-E_and_N.W.A._1988_Skateland_Concert_Poster.jpg 2x" data-file-width="813" data-file-height="1024" /></a><figcaption>Poster for one of N.W.A's first concerts at a Compton skating rink, 1988</figcaption></figure>
<p>At 16, Cube sold his first song to Eric Wright, soon dubbed <a href="/wiki/Eazy-E" title="Eazy-E">Eazy-E</a>, who was forming <a href="/wiki/Ruthless_Records" title="Ruthless Records">Ruthless Records</a> and the musical team <a href="/wiki/N.W.A" title="N.W.A">N.W.A</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/Compton,_California" title="Compton, California">Compton, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jessie_Carney_Smith_2006-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Himself from <a href="/wiki/South_Los_Angeles" title="South Los Angeles">South Central Los Angeles</a>, Cube would be N.W.A's only core member not born in Compton.
</p><p>Upon the success of the song "<a href="/wiki/Boyz-n-the-Hood" title="Boyz-n-the-Hood">Boyz-n-the-Hood</a>"—written by Cube, produced by Dre, and rapped by Eazy-E, helping establish gangsta rap in California—Eazy focused on developing N.W.A,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> which soon gained <a href="/wiki/MC_Ren" title="MC Ren">MC Ren</a>. Cube wrote some of Dre's and nearly all of Eazy's lyrics on N.W.A's official debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton" title="Straight Outta Compton">Straight Outta Compton</a></i>, released in August 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Yet by late 1989, Cube questioned his compensation and N.W.A's management by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Heller" title="Jerry Heller">Jerry Heller</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ice_Cube_2002_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ice_Cube_2002-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cube also wrote most of Eazy-E's debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Eazy-Duz-It" title="Eazy-Duz-It">Eazy-Duz-It</a></i>. He received a total pay of $32,000, and the contract that Heller presented in 1989 did not confirm that he was officially an N.W.A member.<sup id="cite_ref-Attitude70_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Attitude70-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> After leaving the group and its label in December, Cube sued Heller, and the lawsuit was later settled out of court.<sup id="cite_ref-Attitude70_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Attitude70-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In response, N.W.A members attacked Cube on the 1990 EP <i><a href="/wiki/100_Miles_and_Runnin%27" title="100 Miles and Runnin&#39;">100 Miles and Runnin'</a></i>, and on N.W.A's next and final album, <i><a href="/wiki/Niggaz4Life" title="Niggaz4Life">Niggaz4Life</a></i>, in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-:122_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:122-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1989.E2.80.931993:_Early_solo_career.2C_AmeriKKKa.27s_Most_Wanted.2C_Death_Certificate.2C_and_The_Predator"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1989–1993:_Early_solo_career,_AmeriKKKa's_Most_Wanted,_Death_Certificate,_and_The_Predator">1989–1993: Early solo career, <i>AmeriKKKa's Most Wanted</i>, <i>Death Certificate</i>, and <i>The Predator</i></span></h3>
<p>In early 1990, Ice Cube recorded his debut solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/AmeriKKKa%27s_Most_Wanted" title="AmeriKKKa&#39;s Most Wanted">AmeriKKKa's Most Wanted</a></i>, in New York with iconic rap group <a href="/wiki/Public_Enemy_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Enemy (band)">Public Enemy</a>'s production team, <a href="/wiki/The_Bomb_Squad" title="The Bomb Squad">the Bomb Squad</a>. Arriving in May 1990, it was an instant hit, further swelling rap's mainstream integration. Controversial nonetheless, it drew accusations of misogyny and racism. The album introduces Ice Cube's affirmation of black nationalism and ideology of black struggle.
</p><p>Cube appointed <a href="/wiki/Yo-Yo_(rapper)" title="Yo-Yo (rapper)">Yo-Yo</a>, a female rapper and guest on the album, to be the head of his record label, and helped produce her debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Make_Way_for_the_Motherlode" title="Make Way for the Motherlode">Make Way for the Motherlode</a></i>. Also in 1990, Cube followed up with an <a href="/wiki/Extended_Play" class="mw-redirect" title="Extended Play">EP</a>—<i><a href="/wiki/Kill_At_Will" class="mw-redirect" title="Kill At Will">Kill At Will</a></i>—critically acclaimed, and rap's first EP certified <a href="/wiki/Platinum_album" class="mw-redirect" title="Platinum album">Platinum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Allmusic_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allmusic-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His second album <i><a href="/wiki/Death_Certificate_(album)" title="Death Certificate (album)">Death Certificate</a></i> was released in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The album thought to as more focused, yet even more controversial, triggering accusations of anti-white, antisemitic, and misogynist content. The album was split into two themes: the Death Side, "a vision of where we are today", and the Life Side, "a vision of where we need to go". The track "<a href="/wiki/No_Vaseline" title="No Vaseline">No Vaseline</a>" scathingly retorts insults directed at him by N.W.A's <a href="/wiki/100_Miles_and_Runnin%27" title="100 Miles and Runnin&#39;">1990 EP</a> and <a href="/wiki/EFil4zaggin" class="mw-redirect" title="EFil4zaggin">1991 album</a>, which call him a traitor.<sup id="cite_ref-:122_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:122-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Besides calling for hanging Eazy-E as a "house nigga", the track blames N.W.A's manager <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Heller" title="Jerry Heller">Jerry Heller</a> for exploiting the group, mentions that he is a Jew, and calls for his murder.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:7_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Ice Cube contended that he mentioned Heller's ethnicity merely incidentally, not to premise attack, but as news media mention nonwhite assailants' races.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The track "Black Korea", also deemed racist,<sup id="cite_ref-:6_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> was also thought as foreseeing the <a href="/wiki/1992_Los_Angeles_riots" title="1992 Los Angeles riots">1992 Los Angeles riots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Allmusic_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allmusic-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> While controversial, <i>Death Certificate</i> broadened his audience; he toured with <a href="/wiki/Lollapalooza" title="Lollapalooza">Lollapalooza</a> in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-chillin_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chillin-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ice_Cube_and_Da_Lench_Mob_at_the_Ranch_Bowl_1993-02-10_(ticket).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ice_Cube_and_Da_Lench_Mob_at_the_Ranch_Bowl_1993-02-10_%28ticket%29.jpg/220px-Ice_Cube_and_Da_Lench_Mob_at_the_Ranch_Bowl_1993-02-10_%28ticket%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ice_Cube_and_Da_Lench_Mob_at_the_Ranch_Bowl_1993-02-10_%28ticket%29.jpg/330px-Ice_Cube_and_Da_Lench_Mob_at_the_Ranch_Bowl_1993-02-10_%28ticket%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ice_Cube_and_Da_Lench_Mob_at_the_Ranch_Bowl_1993-02-10_%28ticket%29.jpg/440px-Ice_Cube_and_Da_Lench_Mob_at_the_Ranch_Bowl_1993-02-10_%28ticket%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="934" data-file-height="509" /></a><figcaption>A ticket from a 1993 Ice Cube concert in Omaha, Nebraska</figcaption></figure>
<p>Cube's third album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Predator_(1992_album)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Predator (1992 album)">The Predator</a></i>, was released in November 1992. Referring to the <a href="/wiki/1992_Los_Angeles_riots" title="1992 Los Angeles riots">1992 Los Angeles riots</a>, the song "<a href="/wiki/Wicked_(Ice_Cube_song)" title="Wicked (Ice Cube song)">Wicked</a>" opens, "April 29 was power to the people, and we might just see a sequel." <i>The Predator</i> was the first album ever to debut at No. 1 on both the R&amp;B/hip-hop and pop charts. Singles include "<a href="/wiki/It_Was_a_Good_Day" title="It Was a Good Day">It Was a Good Day</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Check_Yo_Self" title="Check Yo Self">Check Yo Self</a>", songs having a "two-part" music video. Generally drawing critical praise, the album is his most successful commercially, over three million copies sold in the US. After this album, Cube's rap audience severely diminished, and never regained the prominence of his first three albums.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this time, Cube began to have numerous features on other artists' songs. In 1992, Cube appeared on <a href="/wiki/Del_the_Funky_Homosapien" title="Del the Funky Homosapien">Del the Funky Homosapien</a>'s debut album <i><a href="/wiki/I_Wish_My_Brother_George_Was_Here" title="I Wish My Brother George Was Here">I Wish My Brother George Was Here</a></i>, on <a href="/wiki/Da_Lench_Mob" title="Da Lench Mob">Da Lench Mob</a>'s debut <i><a href="/wiki/Guerillas_in_tha_Mist" title="Guerillas in tha Mist">Guerillas in tha Mist</a></i>, which he also produced, and on the <a href="/wiki/Kool_G_Rap" title="Kool G Rap">Kool G Rap</a> and <a href="/wiki/DJ_Polo" class="mw-redirect" title="DJ Polo">DJ Polo</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Live_and_Let_Die_(album)" title="Live and Let Die (album)">Two to the Head</a>". In 1993, he worked on <a href="/wiki/Kam_(rapper)" title="Kam (rapper)">Kam</a>'s debut album, and collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Ice-T" title="Ice-T">Ice-T</a> on the track "Last Wordz" on <a href="/wiki/Tupac_Shakur" title="Tupac Shakur">2Pac</a>'s album <i><a href="/wiki/Strictly_4_My_N.I.G.G.A.Z." class="mw-redirect" title="Strictly 4 My N.I.G.G.A.Z.">Strictly 4 My N.I.G.G.A.Z.</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span id="1993.E2.80.931998:_Lethal_Injection_and_forming_Westside_Connection"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1993–1998:_Lethal_Injection_and_forming_Westside_Connection">1993–1998: <i>Lethal Injection</i> and forming Westside Connection</span></h3>
<p>Cube's fourth album, <i><a href="/wiki/Lethal_Injection_(album)" title="Lethal Injection (album)">Lethal Injection</a></i>, came out in late 1993. Here, Cube borrowed from the then-popular <a href="/wiki/G-funk" title="G-funk">G-funk</a> popularized by <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>. Although not received well by critics, the album brought successful singles, including "<a href="/wiki/Really_Doe_(Ice_Cube_song)" title="Really Doe (Ice Cube song)">Really Doe</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Bop_Gun_(One_Nation)" title="Bop Gun (One Nation)">Bop Gun (One Nation)</a>", "<a href="/wiki/You_Know_How_We_Do_It" title="You Know How We Do It">You Know How We Do It</a>", and "What Can I Do?" After this album, Ice Cube effectively lost his rap audience.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following <i>Lethal Injection</i>, Cube focused on films and producing albums of other rappers, including Da Lench Mob, <a href="/wiki/Mack_10" title="Mack 10">Mack 10</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mr._Short_Khop" title="Mr. Short Khop">Mr. Short Khop</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kausion" title="Kausion">Kausion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Allmusic_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allmusic-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 1994, Cube teamed with onetime N.W.A groupmate <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>, who was then leading rap's <a href="/wiki/G-funk" title="G-funk">G-funk</a> subgenre, for the first time since Cube had left the group, and which had disbanded upon Dre's 1991 departure. The result was the Cube and Dre song "<a href="/wiki/Natural_Born_Killaz" title="Natural Born Killaz">Natural Born Killaz</a>", on the <i><a href="/wiki/Murder_Was_The_Case" class="mw-redirect" title="Murder Was The Case">Murder Was The Case</a></i> soundtrack, released by Dre's then-new label, <a href="/wiki/Death_Row_Records" title="Death Row Records">Death Row Records</a>.
</p><p>In 1995, Cube joined Mack 10 and <a href="/wiki/WC_(rapper)" title="WC (rapper)">WC</a> in forming a side trio, the <a href="/wiki/Westside_Connection" title="Westside Connection">Westside Connection</a>. Feeling neglected by East Coast media, a <a href="/wiki/East_Coast%E2%80%93West_Coast_hip_hop_rivalry" title="East Coast–West Coast hip hop rivalry">longstanding issue in rap's bicoastal rivalry</a>, the group aimed to reinforce West pride and resonate with the undervalued. The Westside Connection's first album, <i><a href="/wiki/Bow_Down" title="Bow Down">Bow Down</a></i> (1996), featured tracks like "Bow Down" and "Gangstas Make the World Go 'Round" that reflected the group's objectives. The album was certified <a href="/wiki/RIAA_Certification" class="mw-redirect" title="RIAA Certification">Platinum</a> by year's end. Interpreting rapper <a href="/wiki/Common_(rapper)" title="Common (rapper)">Common</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/I_Used_to_Love_H.E.R." title="I Used to Love H.E.R.">I Used to Love H.E.R.</a>" as a diss of West Coast rap, Cube and the Westside Connection briefly feuded with him, but they resolved amicably in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was also at this time that Cube began collaborating outside the rap genre. In 1997, he worked with <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nine_Inch_Nails" title="Nine Inch Nails">Nine Inch Nails</a> singer <a href="/wiki/Trent_Reznor" title="Trent Reznor">Trent Reznor</a> on a remix of Bowie's "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_Afraid_of_Americans" title="I&#39;m Afraid of Americans">I'm Afraid of Americans</a>". In 1998, Cube was featured on the band <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">Korn</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/Children_of_the_Korn" class="mw-redirect" title="Children of the Korn">Children of the Korn</a>", and joined them on their <a href="/wiki/Family_Values_Tour_1998" title="Family Values Tour 1998">Family Values Tour 1998</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="1998.E2.80.932006:_War_.26_Peace_Vol._1_.26_2_and_Westside_Connection_reunion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1998–2006:_War_&amp;_Peace_Vol._1_&amp;_2_and_Westside_Connection_reunion">1998–2006: <i>War &amp; Peace Vol. 1 &amp; 2</i> and Westside Connection reunion</span></h3>
<p>In November 1998, Cube released his long-awaited fifth solo album <i><a href="/wiki/War_%26_Peace_Vol._1_(The_War_Disc)" title="War &amp; Peace Vol. 1 (The War Disc)">War &amp; Peace Vol. 1 (The War Disc)</a></i>. The delayed sixth album, <i><a href="/wiki/War_%26_Peace_-_Volume_2_(The_Peace_Disc)" class="mw-redirect" title="War &amp; Peace - Volume 2 (The Peace Disc)">Volume 2</a></i>, arrived in 2000. These albums feature the <a href="/wiki/Westside_Connection" title="Westside Connection">Westside Connection</a> and a reunion with his old N.W.A members Dr. Dre and MC Ren. Cube also received a return favor from Korn, as they appeared on his song "Fuck Dying" from <i>Vol. 1</i>. Many fans maintained that these two albums, especially the second, were lesser in quality to his earlier work.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 2000, Cube also joined Dr. Dre, <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Up_in_Smoke_Tour" title="Up in Smoke Tour">Up in Smoke Tour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002, Cube appeared on British DJ <a href="/wiki/Paul_Oakenfold" title="Paul Oakenfold">Paul Oakenfold</a>'s solo debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Bunkka" title="Bunkka">Bunkka</a></i>, on the track "Get Em Up".
</p><p>Released in 2003, Westside Connection's second album, <i><a href="/wiki/Terrorist_Threats" title="Terrorist Threats">Terrorist Threats</a></i>, fared well critically, but saw lesser sales. "Gangsta Nation" (featuring <a href="/wiki/Nate_Dogg" title="Nate Dogg">Nate Dogg</a>), the only single released, was a radio hit. After a rift between Cube and Mack 10 about Cube's film work minimizing the group's touring, the Westside Connection disbanded in 2005.
</p><p>In 2004, Cube featured on the song "<a href="/wiki/Real_Nigga_Roll_Call" title="Real Nigga Roll Call">Real Nigga Roll Call</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Lil_Jon_%26_the_East_Side_Boyz" class="mw-redirect" title="Lil Jon &amp; the East Side Boyz">Lil Jon &amp; the East Side Boyz</a>, the then leaders of rap's <a href="/wiki/Crunk" title="Crunk">crunk</a> subgenre.
</p>
<h3><span id="2006.E2.80.932012:_Laugh_Now.2C_Cry_Later.2C_Raw_Footage.2C_and_I_Am_the_West"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2006–2012:_Laugh_Now,_Cry_Later,_Raw_Footage,_and_I_Am_the_West">2006–2012: <i>Laugh Now, Cry Later</i>, <i>Raw Footage</i>, and <i>I Am the West</i></span></h3>
<p>In 2006, Cube released his seventh solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/Laugh_Now,_Cry_Later" title="Laugh Now, Cry Later">Laugh Now, Cry Later</a></i>, selling 144,000 units in the first week.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lil_Jon" title="Lil Jon">Lil Jon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scott_Storch" title="Scott Storch">Scott Storch</a> produced the lead single, "<a href="/wiki/Why_We_Thugs" title="Why We Thugs">Why We Thugs</a>". In October, Ice Cube was honored at VH1's Annual Hip Hop Honors, and performed it and also the track "Go to Church". Cube soon toured globally in the Straight Outta Compton Tour—accompanied by rapper WC from the Westside Connection—playing in America, Europe, Australia, and Japan.
</p><p>Amid Cube's many features and brief collaborations, September 2007 brought <i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Movies" title="In the Movies">In the Movies</a></i>, a compilation album of Ice Cube songs on soundtracks.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cube's eighth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Raw_Footage" title="Raw Footage">Raw Footage</a></i>, arrived on August 19, 2008, yielding the singles "<a href="/wiki/Gangsta_Rap_Made_Me_Do_It" title="Gangsta Rap Made Me Do It">Gangsta Rap Made Me Do It</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Do_Ya_Thang_(Ice_Cube_song)" title="Do Ya Thang (Ice Cube song)">Do Ya Thang</a>". Also in 2008, Cube helped on <a href="/wiki/Tech_N9ne" title="Tech N9ne">Tech N9ne</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/Killer_(Tech_N9ne_album)" title="Killer (Tech N9ne album)">Blackboy</a>", and was featured on <a href="/wiki/The_Game_(rapper)" title="The Game (rapper)">The Game</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/LAX_(album)" title="LAX (album)">State of Emergency</a>".
</p><p>As a fan of the <a href="/wiki/NFL" class="mw-redirect" title="NFL">NFL</a> football team the Raiders, Cube released in October 2009 a tribute song, "Raider Nation".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, Ice Cube performed at the <a href="/wiki/Gathering_of_the_Juggalos" title="Gathering of the Juggalos">Gathering of the Juggalos</a>, and returned to perform at the 2011 festival.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ice_cube_c.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ice_cube_c.jpg/220px-Ice_cube_c.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ice_cube_c.jpg/330px-Ice_cube_c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ice_cube_c.jpg/440px-Ice_cube_c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="724" data-file-height="481" /></a><figcaption>Ice Cube performing at Metro City Concert Club in October 2010</figcaption></figure>
<p>On September 28, 2010, his ninth solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/I_Am_the_West" title="I Am the West">I Am the West</a></i>, arrived with, Cube says, a direction different from any one of his other albums. Its producers include West Coast veterans like <a href="/wiki/DJ_Quik" title="DJ Quik">DJ Quik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dr._Dre" title="Dr. Dre">Dr. Dre</a>, <a href="/wiki/E-A-Ski" title="E-A-Ski">E-A-Ski</a>, and, after nearly 20 years, again Cube's onetime C.I.A groupmate <a href="/wiki/Sir_Jinx" title="Sir Jinx">Sir Jinx</a>. Offering the single "I Rep That West", the album debuted at #22 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> and sold 22,000 copies in its first week. Also in 2010, Cube signed up-and-coming recording artist named 7Tre The Ghost, deemed likely to be either skipped or given the cookie-cutter treatment by most record companies.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, Cube featured on <a href="/wiki/Daz_Dillinger" title="Daz Dillinger">Daz Dillinger</a>'s <a href="/wiki/D.A.Z._(album)" title="D.A.Z. (album)">song "Iz You Ready to Die"</a> and on <a href="/wiki/DJ_Quik" title="DJ Quik">DJ Quik</a>'s song "Boogie Till You Conk Out".
</p><p>In 2012, Ice Cube recorded a verse for a remix of the <a href="/wiki/Insane_Clown_Posse" title="Insane Clown Posse">Insane Clown Posse</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Chris_Benoit_(song)" title="Chris Benoit (song)">Chris Benoit</a>", from ICP's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mighty_Death_Pop!" title="The Mighty Death Pop!">The Mighty Death Pop!</a></i> album, appearing on the album <i><a href="/wiki/Mike_E._Clark%27s_Extra_Pop_Emporium" title="Mike E. Clark&#39;s Extra Pop Emporium">Mike E. Clark's Extra Pop Emporium</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2012, during <a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">Pepsi</a>'s NFL Anthems campaign, Cube released his second Raiders anthem "Come and Get It".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2012.E2.80.93present:_Everythang.27s_Corrupt_and_forming_Mount_Westmore"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2012–present:_Everythang's_Corrupt_and_forming_Mount_Westmore">2012–present: <i>Everythang's Corrupt</i> and forming Mount Westmore</span></h3>
<p>In November 2012, Cube released more details on his forthcoming, tenth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Everythang%27s_Corrupt" title="Everythang&#39;s Corrupt">Everythang's Corrupt</a></i>. Releasing its title track near the 2012 elections, he added, "You know, this record is for the political heads."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> But the album's release was delayed.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> On February 10, 2014, iTunes brought another single from it, "<a href="/wiki/Sic_Them_Youngins_on_%27Em" class="mw-redirect" title="Sic Them Youngins on &#39;Em">Sic Them Youngins on 'Em</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and a music video followed the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Despite a couple of more song releases, the album's release was delayed even beyond Cube's work on the 2015 film <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton_(2015_film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Straight Outta Compton (2015 film)">Straight Outta Compton</a></i>. After a statement setting release to 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> the album finally arrived on December 7, 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, Cube appeared on <a href="/wiki/MC_Ren" title="MC Ren">MC Ren</a>'s remix "<a href="/wiki/Rebel_Music_(song)" title="Rebel Music (song)">Rebel Music</a>", their first collaboration since the N.W.A reunion in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, Cube joined rappers <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a>, <a href="/wiki/E-40" title="E-40">E-40</a>, <a href="/wiki/Too_Short" title="Too Short">Too Short</a> and formed the supergroup <a href="/wiki/Mt._Westmore" class="mw-redirect" title="Mt. Westmore">Mt. Westmore</a>. The group's debut album was released on June 7, 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_television_career">Film and television career</span></h2>
<p>Since 1991, Ice Cube has acted in nearly 40 films, several of which are highly regarded.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Some of them, such as the 1992 thriller <i><a href="/wiki/Trespass_(1992_film)" title="Trespass (1992 film)">Trespass</a></i> and the 1999 war comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Kings_(1999_film)" title="Three Kings (1999 film)">Three Kings</a></i>, highlight action.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Yet most are comedies, including a few adult-oriented ones, like the <a href="/wiki/Friday_(franchise)" title="Friday (franchise)"><i>Friday</i> franchise</a>, whereas most of these are family-friendly, like the <a href="/wiki/Barbershop_(franchise)" title="Barbershop (franchise)"><i>Barbershop</i> franchise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narrative">Narrative</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Singleton" title="John Singleton">John Singleton</a>'s seminal film <i><a href="/wiki/Boyz_n_the_Hood" title="Boyz n the Hood">Boyz n the Hood</a></i>, released in July 1991, debuted the actor Ice Cube playing Doughboy, a persona that Cube played convincingly.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Later, Cube starred with <a href="/wiki/Ice-T" title="Ice-T">Ice-T</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Paxton" title="Bill Paxton">Bill Paxton</a> in <a href="/wiki/Walter_Hill_(filmmaker)" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter Hill (filmmaker)">Walter Hill</a>'s 1992 thriller film <i><a href="/wiki/Trespass_(1992_film)" title="Trespass (1992 film)">Trespass</a></i>, and in Charles Burnett's 1995 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Glass_Shield" title="The Glass Shield">The Glass Shield</a></i>. Meanwhile, Cube declined to costar with <a href="/wiki/Janet_Jackson" title="Janet Jackson">Janet Jackson</a> in Singleton's 1993 romance <i><a href="/wiki/Poetic_Justice_(film)" title="Poetic Justice (film)">Poetic Justice</a></i>, a role that <a href="/wiki/Tupac_Shakur" title="Tupac Shakur">Tupac Shakur</a> then played.
</p><p>Cube starred as the university student Fudge in Singleton's 1995 film <i><a href="/wiki/Higher_Learning" title="Higher Learning">Higher Learning</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Singleton, encouraging Cube, had reportedly told him, "If you can write a record, you can write a movie."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Cube cowrote the screenplay for the 1995 comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Friday_(1995_film)" title="Friday (1995 film)">Friday</a></i>, based on adult themes, and starred in it with comedian <a href="/wiki/Chris_Tucker" title="Chris Tucker">Chris Tucker</a>. Made with $3.5 million, <i>Friday</i> drew $28&#160;million worldwide. Two sequels, <i><a href="/wiki/Next_Friday" title="Next Friday">Next Friday</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Friday_After_Next" title="Friday After Next">Friday After Next</a></i>, were respectively released in 2000 and 2002.
</p><p>In 1997, playing a South African exiled to America who returns 15 years later, Cube starred in the action thriller <i><a href="/wiki/Dangerous_Ground_(1997_film)" title="Dangerous Ground (1997 film)">Dangerous Ground</a></i>, and had a supporting role in <i><a href="/wiki/Anaconda_(film)" title="Anaconda (film)">Anaconda</a></i>. In 1998, writing again, the director Ice Cube debuted in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Players_Club" title="The Players Club">The Players Club</a></i>. In 1999, he starred alongside <a href="/wiki/George_Clooney" title="George Clooney">George Clooney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Wahlberg" title="Mark Wahlberg">Mark Wahlberg</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Staff_sergeant" title="Staff sergeant">staff sergeant</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Kings_(1999_film)" title="Three Kings (1999 film)">Three Kings</a></i>, set in the immediate aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, whereby the United States attacked Iraq in 1990, an "intelligent" war comedy critically acclaimed.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, Cube starred in <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Bray_(director)" title="Kevin Bray (director)">Kevin Bray</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/All_About_the_Benjamins" title="All About the Benjamins">All About the Benjamins</a></i>, and in <a href="/wiki/Tim_Story" title="Tim Story">Tim Story</a>'s comedy film <i><a href="/wiki/Barbershop_(film)" title="Barbershop (film)">Barbershop</a></i>.
</p><p>In 2004, Cube played in <i><a href="/wiki/Barbershop_2:_Back_in_Business" title="Barbershop 2: Back in Business">Barbershop 2</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Torque_(film)" title="Torque (film)">Torque</a></i>. The next year, he replaced <a href="/wiki/Vin_Diesel" title="Vin Diesel">Vin Diesel</a> in the second installment of the <a href="/wiki/XXX_(film_series)" title="XXX (film series)"><i>XXX</i> film series</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/XXX:_State_of_the_Union" title="XXX: State of the Union">XXX: State of the Union</a></i>, as the main protagonist, which he reprises the character in the third installment and reunited with Diesel 12 years later, <i><a href="/wiki/XXX:_Return_of_Xander_Cage" title="XXX: Return of Xander Cage">XXX: Return of Xander Cage</a></i>. He also appeared in the family comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Are_We_There_Yet%3F_(film)" title="Are We There Yet? (film)">Are We There Yet?</a></i>, which premised his role in its 2007 sequel <i><a href="/wiki/Are_We_Done_Yet%3F" title="Are We Done Yet?">Are We Done Yet?</a></i>. In 2012, Cube appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/21_Jump_Street_(film)" title="21 Jump Street (film)">21 Jump Street</a></i>. He also appeared in its sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/22_Jump_Street" title="22 Jump Street">22 Jump Street</a></i>, in 2014. That year, and then to return in 2016, he played alongside comedian <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Hart" title="Kevin Hart">Kevin Hart</a> in two more Tim Story films, <i><a href="/wiki/Ride_Along_(film)" title="Ride Along (film)">Ride Along</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ride_Along_2" title="Ride Along 2">Ride Along 2</a></i>. Also in 2016, Cube returned for the <a href="/wiki/Barbershop:_The_Next_Cut" title="Barbershop: The Next Cut">third entry in the <i>Barbershop</i> series</a>. And in 2017, Cube starred with <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Day" title="Charlie Day">Charlie Day</a> in the comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Fist_Fight" title="Fist Fight">Fist Fight</a></i>.
</p><p>In October 2021, Ice Cube was set to star in the comedy film <i>Oh Hell No</i> (now titled <i>Stepdude</i><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>) alongside <a href="/wiki/Jack_Black" title="Jack Black">Jack Black</a>, but left the project after refusing to get vaccinated for <a href="/wiki/COVID-19" title="COVID-19">COVID-19</a>. The project would have paid him $9 million.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Documentary">Documentary</span></h3>
<p>In late 2005, Ice Cube and <a href="/wiki/R._J._Cutler" title="R. J. Cutler">R. J. Cutler</a> co-created the six-part documentary series <i><a href="/wiki/Black._White." title="Black. White.">Black. White.</a></i>, carried by cable network <a href="/wiki/FX_(TV_channel)" title="FX (TV channel)">FX</a>.
</p><p>Ice Cube and basketball star <a href="/wiki/LeBron_James" title="LeBron James">LeBron James</a> paired up to pitch a one-hour special to <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> based on James's life.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 11, 2010, ESPN aired Cube's directed documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_L.A." title="Straight Outta L.A.">Straight Outta L.A.</a></i>, examining the interplay of Los Angeles sociopolitics, hip hop, and the Raiders during the 1980s into the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Serial_television">Serial television</span></h3>
<p>Ice Cube's <i><a href="/wiki/Are_We_There_Yet%3F_(TV_series)" title="Are We There Yet? (TV series)">Are We There Yet?</a></i> series premiered on <a href="/wiki/TBS_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="TBS (U.S. TV channel)">TBS</a> on June 2, 2010. It revolves around a family adjusting to the matriarch's new husband, played by <a href="/wiki/Terry_Crews" title="Terry Crews">Terry Crews</a>. On August 16, the show was renewed for 90 more episodes,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> amounting to six seasons. Cube also credits <a href="/wiki/Tyler_Perry" title="Tyler Perry">Tyler Perry</a> for his entrée to TBS.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In front of the television cameras, rather, Cube appeared with Elmo as a 2014 guest on the <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> children's show <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<p>In 1990, a musical associate in the rap group <a href="/wiki/Public_Enemy_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Enemy (band)">Public Enemy</a> introduced Cube to the <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a> (NOI).<sup id="cite_ref-wweek.com_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wweek.com-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> He <a href="/wiki/Converted_to_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Converted to Islam">converted to Islam</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Business_Insider2_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business_Insider2-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> though he denied membership in the NOI,<sup id="cite_ref-chillin_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chillin-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> whose ideology against white people and <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">especially Jews</a> led to its categorization as a <a href="/wiki/Hate_group" title="Hate group">hate group</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Poverty_Law_Center" title="Southern Poverty Law Center">Southern Poverty Law Center</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> However, he readily adopted the group's ideology of <a href="/wiki/Black_nationalism" title="Black nationalism">black nationalism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:8_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> a concept familiar to the hip hop community.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> He nevertheless has claimed to listen his own conscience as a "natural <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-wweek.com_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wweek.com-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> claiming to do so because "it's just [him] and God".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, he expressed support for <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-coorslight_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coorslight-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, he said that he thinks "religion is stupid" in part and explained, "I'm gonna live a long life, and I might change religions three or four times before I die. I'm on the Islam tip—but I'm on the Christian tip, too. I'm on the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> tip as well. Everyone has something to offer to the world."<sup id="cite_ref-:14_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ice Cube has been married to Kimberly Woodruff since April 26, 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> They have four children together; their oldest son <a href="/wiki/O%27Shea_Jackson_Jr." title="O&#39;Shea Jackson Jr.">O'Shea Jackson Jr.</a> (born 1991) portrayed him in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Straight_Outta_Compton_(film)" title="Straight Outta Compton (film)">Straight Outta Compton</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> When asked about the balance between his music and parenting in 2005, Cube discussed teaching his children to question the value of violence depicted in all media, not just song lyrics.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 2016 interview, Cube cited <i><a href="/wiki/Jaws_(film)" title="Jaws (film)">Jaws</a></i> as his favorite film and named "<a href="/wiki/It_Was_a_Good_Day" title="It Was a Good Day">It Was a Good Day</a>" as being among his favorite of his own songs.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Commercially, he has endorsed <a href="/wiki/Coors_Light" title="Coors Light">Coors Light</a> beer and <a href="/wiki/St._Ides" title="St. Ides">St. Ides</a> malt liquor,<sup id="cite_ref-coorslight_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coorslight-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and licensed a clothing line called Solo by Cube. In 2017, he launched <a href="/wiki/Big3" title="Big3">Big3</a>, a <a href="/wiki/3x3_(basketball)" class="mw-redirect" title="3x3 (basketball)">3-on-3</a> basketball league starring former <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">NBA</a> players.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-Semitic_conspiracy_theories_and_allegations_of_Anti-semitism">Anti-Semitic conspiracy theories and allegations of Anti-semitism</span></h3>
<p>At a 1991 press conference promoting his album <i><a href="/wiki/Death_Certificate_(album)" title="Death Certificate (album)">Death Certificate</a></i>, Cube endorsed the Nation of Islam's <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-scholarship" title="Pseudo-scholarship">pseudo-scholarly</a> book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Relationship_Between_Blacks_and_Jews" title="The Secret Relationship Between Blacks and Jews">The Secret Relationship Between Blacks and Jews</a></i>, which falsely claims that European Jews dominated the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> <i>Death Certificate</i> also contains the song "<a href="/wiki/No_Vaseline" title="No Vaseline">No Vaseline</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-:7_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> which uses racial slurs against the other former members of N.W.A and refers to the group's manager <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Heller" title="Jerry Heller">Jerry Heller</a> as "white man", "white boy", "Jew", "devil", "white Jew", and "<a href="/wiki/Cracker_(slur)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cracker (slur)">cracker</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-:10_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>  In response to accusations of racism and <a href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Semitism">anti-Semitism</a>, Cube said in 2008, "I ain't got time to be fuckin' anti-Semitic, anti-this, anti-that, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Korean_sentiment" title="Anti-Korean sentiment">anti-Korean</a>. I ain't got time for that shit. I'm too busy bein' pro-black, you know what I'm saying?"<sup id="cite_ref-:12_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, Cube expressed regret at including the word "Jew" in the lyrics of "No Vaseline" and explained that he intended to attack only Heller and not "the whole Jewish race".<sup id="cite_ref-:10_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> However, that same year, he was sued for allegedly ordering the assault of a <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> During the assault, which he denied instigating, he reportedly uttered anti-Semitic slurs against the rabbi for wearing a <a href="/wiki/Yarmulke" class="mw-redirect" title="Yarmulke">yarmulke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, Marlow Stern wrote an article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Beast" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Beast">Daily Beast</a></i> addressing Cube's "long, disturbing history" of anti-Semitism.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The article was a response to Cube's day-long Twitter posting spree the day before,<sup id="cite_ref-:13_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> during which he promoted Nation of Islam leader <a href="/wiki/Louis_Farrakhan" title="Louis Farrakhan">Louis Farrakhan</a><sup id="cite_ref-:9_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and posted an image of the allegedly anti-Semitic mural <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom_for_Humanity" title="Freedom for Humanity">Freedom for Humanity</a></i> with the caption "Fuck the new normal until they fix the old normal!"<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> He also shared various disproven anti-Semitic conspiracy theories.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Again calling himself "just pro-black" and not "anti-anybody", he dismissed "the hype" and professed that he was just "telling [his] truth".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_nominations">Awards and nominations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_awards">Film awards</span></h3>
<p>Ice Cube has received nominations for several films in the past. To date, he has won two awards:
</p>
<ul><li>2000: Blockbuster Entertainment Award: Favorite Action Team (for <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Kings_(1999_film)" title="Three Kings (1999 film)">Three Kings</a></i>)</li>
<li>2002: MECCA Movie Award: Acting Award</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_awards">Music awards</span></h3>
<ul><li><b>VH1 Hip Hop Honors</b> 2006
<ul><li>2006 Honoree <a href="/wiki/Snoop_Dogg" title="Snoop Dogg">Snoop Dogg</a></li></ul></li>
<li><b>BET Hip-Hop Awards</b> 2009</li>
<li><b>BET Honores</b> 2014</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Walk_of_Fame" title="Hollywood Walk of Fame">Hollywood Walk of Fame</a> star 2017<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
